	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">New York City English</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"><span class="mw-redirectedfrom">&#160;&#160;(Redirected from <a href="/w/index.php?title=New_York_dialect&amp;redirect=no" class="mw-redirect" title="New York dialect">New York dialect</a>)</span></div>
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"New York English" redirects here. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/New_York_English_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="New York English (disambiguation)">New York English (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Regional dialect of American English</div><table class="infobox vevent" style="width:22em"><tbody><tr><th colspan="2" class="above" style="text-align:center;font-size:125%;font-weight:bold;font-size:130%; color: black; background-color:  #c9ffd9;">New York City English</th></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="white-space:nowrap;padding-right:0.65em;">Region</th><td style="line-height:1.3em;"><a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="white-space:nowrap;padding-right:0.65em;">Ethnicity</th><td style="line-height:1.3em;"><a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_New_York_City" title="Demographics of New York City">New Yorkers</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="white-space:nowrap;padding-right:0.65em;"><div style="padding:0.1em 0;line-height:1.2em;"><span class="wrap"><a href="/wiki/Language_family" title="Language family">Language family</a></span></div></th><td style="line-height:1.3em;"><div style="text-align:left;"><a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European</a>
<ul style="line-height:100%; margin-left:1.35em;padding-left:0"><li>
<a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">Germanic</a><ul style="line-height:100%;margin-left:0.45em;padding-left:0;"><li><a href="/wiki/West_Germanic_languages" title="West Germanic languages">West Germanic</a><ul style="line-height:100%;margin-left:0.45em;padding-left:0;"><li><a href="/wiki/Ingvaeonic_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="Ingvaeonic languages">Ingvaeonic</a><ul style="line-height:100%;margin-left:0.45em;padding-left:0;"><li><a href="/wiki/Anglo-Frisian_languages" title="Anglo-Frisian languages">Anglo–Frisian</a><ul style="line-height:100%;margin-left:0.45em;padding-left:0;"><li><a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a><ul style="line-height:100%;margin-left:0.45em;padding-left:0;"><li><a href="/wiki/North_American_English" title="North American English">North American English</a><ul style="line-height:100%;margin-left:0.45em;padding-left:0;"><li><a href="/wiki/American_English" title="American English">American English</a><ul style="line-height:100%;margin-left:0.45em;padding-left:0;"><li><b>New York City English</b></li></ul></li></ul></li></ul></li></ul></li></ul></li></ul></li></ul></li></ul></div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="white-space:nowrap;padding-right:0.65em;"><div style="padding:0.1em 0;line-height:1.2em;"><span class="wrap"><a href="/wiki/Writing_system" title="Writing system">Writing system</a></span></div></th><td style="line-height:1.3em;"><div class="plainlist"><ul><li><a href="/wiki/Latin_script" title="Latin script">Latin</a> (<a href="/wiki/English_alphabet" title="English alphabet">English alphabet</a>)</li><li><a href="/wiki/American_Braille" title="American Braille">American Braille</a></li></ul></div></td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;color: black; background-color:  #c9ffd9;">Language codes</th></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="white-space:nowrap;padding-right:0.65em;"><span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/ISO_639-3" title="ISO 639-3">ISO 639-3</a></span></th><td style="line-height:1.3em;">–</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="white-space:nowrap;padding-right:0.65em;"><a href="/wiki/Glottolog" title="Glottolog">Glottolog</a></th><td style="line-height:1.3em;"><i>None</i></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" class="noprint selfref" style="text-align:center;background-color:#ddddff;padding:0.3em 0.5em;text-align:left;line-height:1.3;"><b>This article contains <a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Alphabet" title="International Phonetic Alphabet">IPA</a> phonetic symbols.</b> Without proper <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA#Rendering_issues" title="Help:IPA">rendering support</a>, you may see <a href="/wiki/Replacement_character" class="mw-redirect" title="Replacement character">question marks, boxes, or other symbols</a> instead of <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">Unicode</a> characters. For an introductory guide on IPA symbols, see <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">Help:IPA</a>.</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>New York City English</b>, or <b>Metropolitan New York English</b>,<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> is a regional <a href="/wiki/Dialect" title="Dialect">dialect</a> of <a href="/wiki/American_English" title="American English">American English</a> spoken by many people in <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a> and much of its surrounding <a href="/wiki/New_York_metropolitan_area" title="New York metropolitan area">metropolitan area</a>. Described by sociolinguist <a href="/wiki/William_Labov" title="William Labov">William Labov</a> as the most recognizable dialect in North America,<sup id="cite_ref-Labov_2006_p._18_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Labov_2006_p._18-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> the dialect is known through its association in the media with many public figures and fictional characters. Its features are most densely concentrated in New York City proper and its immediate suburbs (whose residents often commute to New York City), but they also extend somewhat to the wider metropolitan area and the New York City diaspora in other regions.
</p><p>The dialect is widely known for its pronunciation system, the <a href="/wiki/New_York_accent" title="New York accent">New York accent</a>, which comprises a number of both <a href="/wiki/Conservative_(linguistics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Conservative (linguistics)">conservative</a> and innovative features. Major features of the accent include a <a href="/wiki/High_vowel" class="mw-redirect" title="High vowel">high</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diphthong" title="Diphthong">gliding</a> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ɔ/</span> vowel (in words like <i>talk</i> and <i>caught</i>); a <a href="/wiki/Phonemic_split" class="mw-redirect" title="Phonemic split">split</a> of the "short a" vowel <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/æ/</span> into two separate sounds; variable <a href="/wiki/Rhoticity_in_English" title="Rhoticity in English">dropping of <i>r</i> sounds</a>; and a lack of the <i><a href="/wiki/Cot-caught_merger" class="mw-redirect" title="Cot-caught merger">cot–caught</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Mary-marry-merry_merger" class="mw-redirect" title="Mary-marry-merry merger">Mary–marry–merry</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Hurry-furry_merger" class="mw-redirect" title="Hurry-furry merger">hurry–furry</a></i> mergers.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_York_City_English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The origins of many of New York City English's diverse features are probably not recoverable. New York City English, largely with the same major pronunciation system popularly recognized today, was first reproduced in literature and also scientifically documented in the 1890s.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> It was then, and still mostly is, associated with ethnically diverse <a href="/wiki/European_Americans" title="European Americans">European-American</a> native-English speakers. The dialect likely evolved from an older English variety that encompassed much of the larger <a href="/wiki/Mid-Atlantic_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Mid-Atlantic States">Mid-Atlantic region</a>, including the <a href="/wiki/Delaware_Valley" title="Delaware Valley">Delaware Valley</a> (whose <a href="/wiki/Mid-Atlantic_American_English" title="Mid-Atlantic American English">own distinct dialect</a> centers around <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia,_Pennsylvania" class="mw-redirect" title="Philadelphia, Pennsylvania">Philadelphia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Baltimore,_Maryland" class="mw-redirect" title="Baltimore, Maryland">Baltimore</a>), since all of this larger region's dialects still share certain key features, including a <a href="/wiki/Close_vowel" title="Close vowel">high</a> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><span class="nowrap">/ɔ/</span></span> vowel with a <a href="/wiki/Diphthong" title="Diphthong">glide</a> (sometimes called the <i>aww</i> vowel) as well as a <a href="/wiki/Phonemic_split" class="mw-redirect" title="Phonemic split">phonemic split</a> of the short <i>a</i> vowel, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><span class="nowrap">/æ/</span></span> (making <i>gas</i> and <i>gap</i>, for example, have different vowels sounds)—New York City's split not identical though to Philadelphia's. Linguist <a href="/wiki/William_Labov" title="William Labov">William Labov</a> has pointed out that a similarly-structured (though differently pronounced) <a href="/wiki/Trap%E2%80%93bath_split" title="Trap–bath split">split</a> is found today in both the standard and London-area accents of England, indicating the likely origin of the New York split.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;a&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>New York City became an urban economic power in the eighteenth century, with the city's financial elites maintaining close ties with the British Empire even after the Revolutionary War. According to Labov, New York speakers' <a href="/wiki/Rhoticity_in_English" title="Rhoticity in English">loss of the <i>r</i> sound after vowels</a> (incidentally, not found in the nearby Delaware Valley) is thus an imitation of the <a href="/wiki/Received_pronunciation" class="mw-redirect" title="Received pronunciation">prestigious British feature</a>, consistently starting among the upper classes in New York City in the nineteenth century before spreading to other socioeconomic classes.<sup id="cite_ref-Labov_et_al.,_2006,_p._47_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Labov_et_al.,_2006,_p._47-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> After World War II, social perceptions reversed, and the <i>r</i>-preserving (rhotic) pronunciation became the prestige norm throughout the whole United States. Thus, what was once an upper-class feature only remained strong among vernacular speakers, due to the loss of Britain's imperial status, the national embracing of non-East Coast features,<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> and widespread postwar migrations of rhotic speakers directly to New York from other regions of the country.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Today, most New York City English is variably rhotic, a remnant of the older non-rhotic pronunciation.
</p><p>Other features of the dialect, such as the dental <i>d</i> and <i>t</i>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Th-stopping" title="Th-stopping"><i>th</i>-stopping</a>, likely come from contact with foreign languages, particularly Italian and Yiddish, brought into New York City through its huge immigration waves of Europeans during and before the early twentieth century. Grammatical structures, such as the lack of inversion in indirect questions, similarly suggest contact with immigrant languages, plus several words common in the city are derived from such foreign languages.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Influence_on_other_dialects">Influence on other dialects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_York_City_English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Influence on other dialects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Philadelphians born in the twentieth century exhibit a short-<i>a</i> split system that some linguists regard as a simplification of the very similar New York City short-<i>a</i> split.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Younger Philadelphians, however, are retreating from many of the traditional features shared in common with New York City.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Due to an influx of immigrants from New York City and neighboring New Jersey to <a href="/wiki/South_Florida" title="South Florida">southern Florida</a>, some resident southern Floridians now speak with an accent reminiscent of a New York accent. Additionally, as a result of social and commercial contact between the two cities and the influx of immigrants from the same countries,<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> the traditional accent of <a href="/wiki/New_Orleans,_Louisiana" class="mw-redirect" title="New Orleans, Louisiana">New Orleans, Louisiana</a>, known locally as "<a href="/wiki/Yat_(New_Orleans)" class="mw-redirect" title="Yat (New Orleans)">Yat</a>", bears distinctive similarities with the New York accent, including the (moribund) <a href="/wiki/Coil%E2%80%93curl_merger" class="mw-redirect" title="Coil–curl merger">coil–curl merger</a>, raising of <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ɔ/</span> to <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ɔə]</span>, a similar split in the short-<i>a</i> system, and <a href="/wiki/Th-stopping" title="Th-stopping"><i>th</i>-stopping</a>. Therefore, older New York City English also presumably influenced dialect evolution in <a href="/wiki/Cincinnati,_Ohio" class="mw-redirect" title="Cincinnati, Ohio">Cincinnati, Ohio</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Albany,_New_York" title="Albany, New York">Albany, New York</a>, whose older speakers in particular may still exhibit a short-<i>a</i> split system that appears to be an expanded or generalized variant of the New York City short-<i>a</i> system. Certain New York City dialect features also appear in <a href="/wiki/New_York_Latino_English" title="New York Latino English">New York Latino English</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Recent_developments">Recent developments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_York_City_English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Recent developments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Though <a href="/wiki/William_Labov" title="William Labov">William Labov</a> argued in 2010 that the New York accent is basically stable at the moment,<sup id="cite_ref-Labov_2010_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Labov_2010-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> some recent studies have revealed a trend of recession in certain features of the accent, especially among younger speakers from middle-class or higher backgrounds. Documented loss of New York City accent features includes the loss of: the <a href="/wiki/Coil%E2%80%93curl_merger" class="mw-redirect" title="Coil–curl merger">coil–curl merger</a> (now almost completely extinct), non-rhoticity, and the extremely raised long vowel <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ɔ]</span> (as in <i>talk</i>, <i>cough</i>, or <i>law</i>). Researchers proposed that the motivation behind these recessive trends is the stigmatization against the typical New York accent since the mid-1900s as being associated with a poorer or working-class background, often also corresponding with particular ethnic identities. While earlier projects detected trends of emphasizing New York accents as part of a process of social identification, recent researches attribute the loss of typical accent features to in-group ethnic distancing. In other words, many of the young generations of ethnic groups who formerly were the most representative speakers of the accent are currently avoiding its features in order to not stand out socially and/or ethnically.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Pronunciation">Pronunciation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_York_City_English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Pronunciation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/New_York_accent" title="New York accent">New York accent</a></div>
<p>The pronunciation of New York City English, most popularly acknowledged by the term "New York accent", is readily noticed and stereotyped, garnering considerable attention in American culture.<sup id="cite_ref-Labov_et_al.,_2006,_p._233_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Labov_et_al.,_2006,_p._233-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Some well-known phonological features include its traditional <a href="/wiki/Rhoticity_in_English" title="Rhoticity in English">dropping of <i>r</i></a>, a <a href="/wiki//%C3%A6/_tensing" class="mw-redirect" title="/æ/ tensing">short-<i>a</i> split system</a> (in which, for example, the <i>a</i> in <i>gas</i> is not <a href="/wiki/Assonance" title="Assonance">assonant</a> to the <i>a</i> in <i>gap</i>), a <a href="/wiki/Close_vowel" title="Close vowel">high</a> <a href="/wiki/Diphthong" title="Diphthong">gliding</a> vowel in words like <i>talk</i>, <i>thought</i>, <i>all</i>, etc. (and thus an absence of the <a href="/wiki/Cot%E2%80%93caught_merger" title="Cot–caught merger">cot–caught merger</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-Labov_et_al.,_2006,_p._233_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Labov_et_al.,_2006,_p._233-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> absence of the <a href="/wiki/Mary%E2%80%93marry%E2%80%93merry_merger" class="mw-redirect" title="Mary–marry–merry merger">Mary–marry–merry merger</a>, and the stigmatized (and largely now-extinct) <a href="/wiki/Coil%E2%80%93curl_merger" class="mw-redirect" title="Coil–curl merger">coil–curl merger</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Vocabulary_and_grammar">Vocabulary and grammar</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_York_City_English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Vocabulary and grammar">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Regional_vocabularies_of_American_English" class="mw-redirect" title="Regional vocabularies of American English">Regional vocabularies of American English</a></div>
<p>There are some words used mainly in Greater New York City. For instance, a "stoop" (from the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_language" title="Dutch language">Dutch</a> word <i>"stoep"</i>) is the front steps of a building. In the black and Latino communities, the word <i>punk</i> tends to be used as a synonym for "weak", "someone unwilling or unable to defend himself" or perhaps "loser", though it appears to descend from an outdated <a href="/wiki/African-American_English" title="African-American English">African-American English</a> meaning of <i>male receptive participant in anal sex</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>New Yorkers tend to say they stand <i>on</i> line, whereas most other American-English speakers tend to stand <i>in</i> line.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Small convenience stores have, in recent decades, often been called <i>bodegas</i>, from the Spanish term originally meaning "a wine storehouse" via the <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rican_Spanish" title="Puerto Rican Spanish">Puerto Rican Spanish</a> term for "small store; corner store"; by extension, "<a href="/wiki/Bodega_cat" title="Bodega cat">bodega cats</a>" is the term for the cats that inhabit such establishments.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> These small stores may also be called <i>delis</i>, which is the short form of <i><a href="/wiki/Delicatessen" title="Delicatessen">delicatessens</a></i>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Conversational_styles">Conversational styles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_York_City_English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Conversational styles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>New York speakers have some unique conversational styles. Linguistics professor Deborah Tannen notes in a <i>New York Times</i> article it has "an emphasis to involve the other person, rather than being considerate. It would be asking questions as a show of interest in the other person, whereas in other parts of [the] country, people don't ask because it might put the person on the spot." New Yorkers "stand closer, talk louder, and leave shorter pauses between exchanges," Tannen said. "I call it 'cooperative overlap'. It's a way of showing interest and enthusiasm, but it's often mistaken for interrupting by people from elsewhere in the country." On the other hand, linguist William Labov demurs, "there's nothing known to linguists about 'normal New York City conversation'".<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notable_speakers">Notable speakers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_York_City_English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Notable speakers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The accent has a strong presence in media; pioneer <a href="/wiki/Variation_(linguistics)" title="Variation (linguistics)">variationist sociolinguist</a> <a href="/wiki/William_Labov" title="William Labov">William Labov</a> describes it as the most recognizable variety of <a href="/wiki/North_American_English" title="North American English">North American English</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Labov_2006_p._18_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Labov_2006_p._18-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> The following famous people are native New York speakers, demonstrating typical features of the accent.
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fictional_characters">Fictional characters</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_York_City_English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Fictional characters">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Many fictional characters in popular films and television shows have used New York City English, whether or not the actors portraying them are native speakers of the dialect. Some examples are listed below.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geographic_boundaries">Geographic boundaries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_York_City_English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Geographic boundaries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="New_York_State">New York State</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_York_City_English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: New York State">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>New York City English is confined to a geographically small but densely populated area, including all <a href="/wiki/Five_boroughs" class="mw-redirect" title="Five boroughs">five boroughs</a> of New York City, but not all of <a href="/wiki/New_York_State" class="mw-redirect" title="New York State">New York State</a>; an entirely <a href="/wiki/Inland_Northern_American_English" title="Inland Northern American English">separate dialect</a> predominates in <a href="/wiki/Central_New_York" title="Central New York">central</a> and <a href="/wiki/Western_New_York" title="Western New York">western New York State</a>, especially along the <a href="/wiki/Great_Lakes_region" title="Great Lakes region">Great Lakes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Labov_et_al._2006_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Labov_et_al._2006-158">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> However, New York City English does extend beyond the city proper, including in western <a href="/wiki/Long_Island" title="Long Island">Long Island</a> (although the boundaries there are not clearly established).<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> Moreover, the English of the <a href="/wiki/Hudson_Valley" title="Hudson Valley">Hudson Valley</a> forms a continuum of speakers who gather more features of New York City English the closer they are to the city itself;<sup id="cite_ref-Labov_et_al._2006_158-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Labov_et_al._2006-158">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> some of the dialect's features may be heard as far north as the city of <a href="/wiki/Albany,_New_York" title="Albany, New York">Albany</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="New_Jersey">New Jersey</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_York_City_English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: New Jersey">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Gateway_Region" title="Gateway Region">northeast quarter</a> of <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey" title="New Jersey">New Jersey</a>, prominently <a href="/wiki/Bergen_County,_New_Jersey" title="Bergen County, New Jersey">Bergen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hudson_County,_New_Jersey" title="Hudson County, New Jersey">Hudson</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Essex_County,_New_Jersey" title="Essex County, New Jersey">Essex</a> counties, including the cities <a href="/wiki/Weehawken,_New_Jersey" title="Weehawken, New Jersey">Weehawken</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hoboken,_New_Jersey" title="Hoboken, New Jersey">Hoboken</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jersey_City,_New_Jersey" title="Jersey City, New Jersey">Jersey City</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bayonne,_New_Jersey" title="Bayonne, New Jersey">Bayonne</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Newark,_New_Jersey" title="Newark, New Jersey">Newark</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> plus Middlesex and Monmouth Counties, are all within the <a href="/wiki/New_York_City_metropolitan_area" class="mw-redirect" title="New York City metropolitan area">New York City metropolitan area</a> and thus also home to the major features of New York City English. With the exception of New York City's immediate neighbors like Jersey City and Newark,<sup id="cite_ref-Labov_et_al.,_2006,_p._47_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Labov_et_al.,_2006,_p._47-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> the New York metropolitan dialect as spoken in New Jersey is <a href="/wiki/Rhotic_and_non-rhotic_accents" class="mw-redirect" title="Rhotic and non-rhotic accents">rhotic</a> (or fully <i>r</i>-pronouncing), so that, whereas a Brooklynite might pronounce "over there" something like "ovah theah/deah" <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[oʊvə ˈd̪ɛə]</span>, an <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth,_New_Jersey" title="Elizabeth, New Jersey">Elizabeth</a> native might say "over there/dare" <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[oʊvɚ ˈd̪ɛɚ]</span>. <i><a href="/wiki/The_Atlas_of_North_American_English" title="The Atlas of North American English">The Atlas of North American English</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/William_Labov" title="William Labov">William Labov</a> et al. shows that the New York City <a href="/wiki/Phonological_history_of_English_short_A" class="mw-redirect" title="Phonological history of English short A">short-<i>a</i></a> pattern has diffused to many <i>r</i>-pronouncing communities in northern New Jersey like <a href="/wiki/Rutherford,_New_Jersey" title="Rutherford, New Jersey">Rutherford</a> (Labov's birthplace) and <a href="/wiki/North_Plainfield,_New_Jersey" title="North Plainfield, New Jersey">North Plainfield</a>. However, in these communities, the <a href="/wiki/Function_word" title="Function word">function word</a> constraint is lost and the <a href="/wiki/Syllable#Structure" title="Syllable">open syllable</a> constraint is variable.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Notable_speakers_2">Notable speakers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_York_City_English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Notable speakers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The following is a list of notable lifelong native speakers of the <a href="/wiki/Rhotic_accent" class="mw-redirect" title="Rhotic accent">rhotic</a> New York City English of northeastern New Jersey:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Jon_Bon_Jovi" title="Jon Bon Jovi">Jon Bon Jovi</a><sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Danny_DeVito" title="Danny DeVito">Danny DeVito</a><sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/James_Gandolfini" title="James Gandolfini">James Gandolfini</a><sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ed_Harris" title="Ed Harris">Ed Harris</a><sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/William_Labov" title="William Labov">William Labov</a><sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ray_Liotta" title="Ray Liotta">Ray Liotta</a><sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup></li>

<p>And these are non-rhotic speakers from New Jersey.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_York_City_English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-4">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFLabovAshBoberg2006">Labov, Ash &amp; Boberg (2006)</a>, p.&#160;173: "In NYC and the Mid-Atlantic region, short-a is split into a tense and lax class. There is reason to believe that the tense class <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/æh/</span> descends from the British <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ah/</span> or 'broad-a' class."</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="External_links">External links</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=New_York_City_English&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: External links">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul><li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.ic.arizona.edu/~lsp/Northeast/NewYorkEnglish/nyphon.html">Varieties of English: New York City phonology</a> from the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Arizona" title="University of Arizona">University of Arizona</a>'s Language Samples Project</li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.ling.upenn.edu/phono-atlas/Icslp4.html">A paper by Labov on dialect diversity, including information on NY dialect phonology</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template"><span style="white-space: nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Link_rot" title="Wikipedia:Link rot"><span title="&#160;Dead link since December 2017">permanent dead link</span></a></i>&#93;</span></sup></li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20050309100939/http://qcpages.qc.edu/LCD/faculty/newman/NYLE.html">The New York Latino English Project</a> The site of the New York Latino English project, which studies the native English spoken by New York Latinos.</li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20050924090853/http://alt-usage-english.org/audio_archive.shtml">A site with samples of speech in various dialects, including New York</a></li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20080227042348/http://www.amny.com/news/local/am-accentside0225,0,6082176.story">AM New York's feature on the New York accent</a></li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=uBiIrBsV2rY">Video</a> on <a href="/wiki/YouTube" title="YouTube">YouTube</a> Demonstration of NYC English raised and tensed /ɔ/, i.e., the THOUGHT vowel in words like 'coffee' and 'sausage.'</li></ul>
